How skills-based hiring is changing degree requirementsskills based hiring KD 12 · vol 880

Why employers are moving away from degree screens

  • In 2024, about 45% of organizations reportedly stopped requiring a bachelor’s degree for some jobs, in addition to the 55% that had already done so in 2023.[11]171nationalskillscoalition.org
  • In a poll of 800 U.S. employers, about 80% said they would prefer a person with relevant experience to a college graduate when hiring.[11]171nationalskillscoalition.org
  • Named employers reported as having removed degree requirements include IBM, Google, Delta Air Lines, and Bank of America.[11]171nationalskillscoalition.org
  • Removing outdated degree requirements, arbitrary experience thresholds, and screening steps that add time without improving quality can widen the talent pool and reduce hiring bottlenecks.[12]14uniformguidelines.com
  • The share of job postings requiring a college degree rose to 19.8% in April 2022, then declined again.our own page
  • Between 2020 and 2022, degree requirements increased in 19 of the 47 occupational groups analyzed by Indeed.our own page
  • For jobs where a degree is not necessary, requiring one can disadvantage lower-income applicants and create adverse-impact risk.[22]35appam.org
  • The talent market is tight and getting tighter. When 4 in 5 organizations say they can’t find candidates with the skills they need, the old playbook of filtering on degrees is a liability, not a safeguard. Skills-based hiring gives you a wider pool and better signal on who will actually perform. A pattern I keep seeing with TA teams at large organizations is this: the degree requirement on a job description was never based on performance data. It was inherited from a posting written years ago, or copied from a competitor’s listing. Nobody ever tested whether degreed hires outperformed non-degreed ones. When those teams finally run the analysis, they almost always find the degree was doing very little predictive work. The stakes are real. SHRM’s 2025 data shows 69% of organizations are struggling to fill full-time roles. You don’t have the luxury of an artificial filter that cuts out two-thirds of the workforce before the first conversation happens. Shifting to skills-based evaluation is the practical response to a market that won’t ease up on its own.33testlify.com
  • The honest answer is: not well enough to justify using it as a primary filter. Decades of industrial-organizational psychology research show that work samples and cognitive ability tests outperform educational credentials as predictors of on-the-job performance. A degree signals that someone completed a program. It doesn’t tell you whether they can solve the specific problems your team faces today. There are roles where formal education carries genuine weight. A licensed pharmacist, a structural engineer, a CPA, these all require credentials tied to legal and safety standards. But for the majority of roles at a 1,000-plus employee organization, from operations analysts to account managers to content strategists, the degree is a proxy, and it’s an expensive one. Expensive because it costs you candidates. HBR’s 2024 analysis cites 2023 US Census Bureau data showing degree requirements eliminate nearly two-thirds of the US workforce from consideration. Think about that number for a moment. You’re pre-screening out roughly 65% of potential applicants before they write a single word of an application, based on a credential that doesn’t reliably predict how they’ll perform once hired. That’s not rigor. That’s habit dressed up as rigor.33testlify.com
  • The table below summarizes the key differences across the dimensions that matter most to TA leaders managing enterprise-scale hiring. Dimension Degree-first hiring Skills-based hiring Candidate pool size Cuts out roughly two-thirds of the US workforce (HBR 2024) Open to all qualified candidates regardless of credential Primary filter Educational credential (lagging indicator) Validated assessment score (leading indicator) Predictive validity for job performance Low to moderate for most roles High when assessments are role-mapped and validated Retention of hires Baseline Non-degreed hires retain at 10 ppts higher at skills-leader firms (Burning Glass / HBS, 2023) Diversity of shortlist Skewed toward candidates with access to 4-year degree programs More representative of the actual workforce Recruiter time per hire High: manual resume review at volume Lower: scored profiles replace raw resume stacks Bias surface area High: school name, GPA, institution prestige all introduce bias Lower: score replaces credential signal as primary sort Implementation timeline Not applicable (default mode) Job description rewrites: 1 day. Assessment deployment: under 1 week. The contrast on diversity and retention is where most TA leaders I speak with have the sharpest reaction. They expected the pool size argument. The retention data surprises them more. You’re not just getting more candidates: you’re getting candidates who stay.33testlify.com
  • When you remove unnecessary degree requirements or strict experience thresholds, the pool of qualified candidates grows dramatically. LinkedIn’s analysis found that by focusing on skills over rigid criteria, companies can increase their talent pool by up to 19X for certain roles. This is a staggering figure as it means positions that once had a very limited candidate supply (due to overly restrictive job specs) can suddenly be filled much faster because more people are deemed eligible to apply. Not only is the talent pool larger, but it’s also more diverse in background. As noted, skills-based hiring opens the door to non-traditional candidates who may bring unique perspectives. Companies report direct improvements in diversity outcomes after adopting this approach. For example, LinkedIn data shows 25% increases in gender diversity at companies that embraced skills-first hiring practices. There’s also a bias reduction component that drives these results. Removing elitist filters (like “must have Ivy League education” or “must have Big-5 experience”) helps level the playing field. Over time, this creates a richer, more inclusive team. McKinsey famously found that companies in the top quartile for diversity are 27% more likely to financially outperform their peers.19testlify.com
  • By removing unnecessary barriers like degree requirements, businesses can tap into a wider and more diverse talent pool. According to LinkedIn, skills-based hiring can increase the proportion of Gen Z candidates by 10x and Millennials by 9x. Skills-based hiring increases women’s representation in underrepresented roles by 24% compared to traditional recruitment practices.  When hiring focuses on ability rather than pedigree, more opportunities open up for underrepresented groups.28testlify.com
  • No, skills-based hiring does not require degrees.  While college degrees can be valuable, many roles do not necessarily require them. It promotes inclusivity by creating opportunities for individuals without degrees, ensuring job access is based on skills rather than formal education.  In countries, especially in the U.S., the cost of higher education is skyrocketing, most candidates opt for other means of education than traditional ones. This prompts a global appreciation for skills-based hiring.  Companies worldwide are facing trouble filling roles, specifically ones that require technical expertise. The talent shortage is no longer a minor issue; globally, around 80% of employers report challenges filling open positions.  Also, the employer-employee contract has significantly changed. Employees need more flexibility, and skills-based recruitment offers exactly that. Candidates no longer need to visit the company to conduct interviews.  As education pathways diversify and the demand for specialized skills rises, this approach is the future. Either way, having a degree or formal education doesn’t guarantee skilled workers. Boeing, for example, is one of the companies that is not satisfied with college graduates and is now testing the waters with skills-based hiring.28testlify.com
  • LinkedIn research shows that employers significantly increase the number of eligible candidates for a typical job when they prioritise skills over traditional job titles or degrees. - On average, talent pools grow 6.1 times when hiring is based on skills. - The United States sees the biggest increase, with a 15.9x expansion in eligible candidates. - Brazil follows with a 12.9x increase, while Mexico (10.4x) and the UK (8.4x) also experience significant growth. - Smaller increases are seen in Slovenia (1.1x) and Croatia (1.7x), while countries like Ireland, Singapore, and Switzerland see 4.3x to 4.4x increases. The trend suggests that skills-based hiring can dramatically broaden talent pools in countries where many workers share transferable skills, making recruitment more inclusive and efficient.28testlify.com
  • This means that skills-based hiring can dramatically expand the number of eligible candidates in various industries up to 22 times in some cases by focusing on skills rather than job titles. Industries where skills overlap across different roles, see the biggest increases in available talent. In India, the Real Estate and Equipment Rental Services industry could expand its talent pool by 86.4x. In the U.S., Accommodation and Food Services could see a 34.9x increase in candidates. In France, the Construction sector could experience a 23.4x expansion. This shows that traditional hiring methods often overlook skilled candidates simply because they don’t have the “right” job title. A skills-based approach helps companies find more qualified talent while making hiring more efficient and inclusive.28testlify.com

What skills-based screening and sourcing look like in practice

  • Skills-first systems can recognize capability gained through work experience, apprenticeships, certifications, or self-study rather than only through formal education.[14]40starred.com
  • Employers can broaden access by promoting telework and hybrid options, making postings screen-reader friendly, avoiding images that contain embedded text, and tracking outreach by skills-based criteria rather than traditional credentials.[14]40starred.com
  • Maintaining candidate relationships through structured follow-up, virtual career information sessions, employee referrals, retiree networks, alumni networks, and professional associations can support sourcing beyond conventional pipelines.[14]40starred.com
  • Maintaining candidate relationships through structured follow-up, virtual career information sessions, employee referrals, retiree networks, alumni networks, and professional associations can support sourcing beyond conventional pipelines.[14]40starred.com
  • Skills-based screening should still distinguish meaningful credentials from weak signals.our own page
  • The evidence identifies several indicators of credential quality: substantial associated job opportunities; clearly defined competencies aligned with job skill requirements; evidence of improved employment and earnings outcomes; job-quality indicators such as benefits, sick leave, and dependable hours; stackability into further education or training; and portability across employers and job opportunities.[15]37kellyservices.com
  • The evidence identifies several indicators of credential quality: substantial associated job opportunities; clearly defined competencies aligned with job skill requirements; evidence of improved employment and earnings outcomes; job-quality indicators such as benefits, sick leave, and dependable hours; stackability into further education or training; and portability across employers and job opportunities.[15]37kellyservices.com
  • The National Skills Coalition describes a quality credential as one connected to substantial job opportunities; grounded in clearly defined competencies aligned with job requirements; associated with improved employment and earnings outcomes; linked to job-quality indicators such as health or retirement benefits, sick leave, and dependable hours; stackable into additional education or training; and portable across employers and job opportunities.[15]37kellyservices.com
  • Resume and credential screening can restrict hiring to a limited pool of credential-holders, while skills-based selection seeks actual job fit.[43]157master-hr.com
  • IBM removed degree requirements from more than 50% of U.S. job openings,our own page
  • and Google, Apple, and Tesla have publicly stated that degrees are not required for many roles;our own page
  • These shifts indicate that major employers are treating skills-first hiring as a talent-access and selection approach, not solely an equity initiative.[43]157master-hr.com
  • Skills-based hiring evaluates demonstrable abilities, work samples, and structured assessments instead of relying on a four-year degree or alma mater.[1]7pin.com
  • Across more than 37,000 recruiter search sessions on Pin, 79.7% of searches filtered on skills, while 32.4% used any education filter.[1]7pin.com
  • Among searches that did use an education filter, 39.6% explicitly permitted work-experience equivalency in place of a degree.[1]7pin.com
  • In 29,000-plus job descriptions analyzed on Pin, 69.2% omitted degree-gating language.[1]7pin.com
  • In role-requirement notes under 500 characters, the no-degree share was 94.2%.[1]7pin.com
  • When recruiters set a minimum-experience requirement, the median was five years, with a 25th percentile of three years and a 75th percentile of seven years, making years of experience the de facto skills proxy at the sourcing stage.[1]7pin.com
  • A skills-based process can reduce reliance on shorthand signals such as years of experience, degree qualifications, recognizable employers, and familiar job titles.our own page
  • It instead evaluates candidates against clearly defined, job-related capabilities and common criteria.[14]8ere.net
  • New tools driven by AI can parse resumes and online profiles to infer candidates’ skills (including those gained through non-traditional paths). AI-based sourcing platforms can match candidates to roles based on skill similarity, even if titles or industries differ. Additionally, some companies are building skills databases or talent marketplaces internally, tagging employees and applicants with granular skill profiles. Such data-driven approaches help identify top talent by focusing on capability rather than pedigree.11testlify.com
  • A growth mindset is a strong predictor that an employee will acquire whatever new skills are needed down the line. Hiring for potential as much as current skill sets can ensure you snag high-ceiling talent. Recruiters should look for evidence of upskilling, such as certifications, online courses, hackathon participation, or progression into new skills over time. Interview questions can probe how candidates have learned new technologies or skills in the past. Many forward-thinking companies even give assessment opportunities to candidates to demonstrate skills they might not have formal experience in, tapping into that pool of high-potential learners. By implementing these strategies, recruiters can more effectively identify which candidates truly have the right stuff for the job.11testlify.com
  • As companies increasingly shift toward skill-based hiring, traditional hiring methods—such as resumes and cover letters—are no longer enough to secure a job. While they still play an important role in candidate screening, they provide limited information on candidates’ suitability for the job. After all, just because someone uses a well-crafted resume template that highlights their qualifications and achievements doesn’t always mean they are the perfect fit for the role. In fact, many candidates exaggerate their skills and experience to seem more qualified or embellish their cover letters to appear more motivated than they genuinely are. Such dishonesty can make talent assessment challenging because it gives an inaccurate view of the candidate. Not to mention, resume and cover letter screening can trigger unconscious biases, clouding recruiters’ judgment and impacting their hiring decisions. To avoid such biases and ensure fair and efficient recruitment processes, companies increasingly utilize recruitment technology, data-driven strategies, and AI in hiring.12testlify.com
  • Yes, many employers are removing degree requirements from job postings. From 2014 to 2023, the number of roles that dropped degree requirements nearly quadrupled, although there was a slight decline after a peak in 2022.  Companies such as Koch Industries, Walmart, Apple, General Motors, Target, Cigna, Tyson Foods, ExxonMobil, and Yelp, along with government employers like the State of Minnesota and the City of Denver, have eliminated degree requirements and hired more non-degreed workers. However, adopting skills-based hiring isn’t just about removing degree requirements from job descriptions. Companies must implement structured hiring practices to assess candidates based on their skills effectively.  While progress has been slow, companies embracing skills-based hiring see measurable benefits. For example, non-degreed employees in roles that previously required degrees have a retention rate 10 percentage points higher than their degree-holding colleagues. Additionally, these workers experience an average salary increase of 25%. This shift benefits both employers and workers, making skills-based hiring a valuable approach. Despite its slow adoption, research suggests that it has the potential to expand significantly, with at least 250,000 more jobs becoming eligible for skills-based hiring each year. How public-sector skills-based hiring policies are spreading

  • In the years after such action, 22 of the 25 states and territories that adopted these changes increased their share of job postings without degree requirements faster than states and territories that had not promoted skills-based hiring.[2]5shrm.org
  • One analysis estimates that, in states with such commitments, degree requirements in state-government job postings fell by 2.5 percentage points for each additional year of policy exposure.[23]172opm.gov
  • The potential reach is substantial: if skills-based action within state and territorial governments catalyzed broader public-sector change, up to 22.0 million jobs, or 14.1% of U.S. jobs, could be affected.[2]5shrm.org
  • Looking only at public-administration roles, excluding public hospitals, educational institutions, and the military, the estimated reach is 10.4 million jobs, or 6.7% of U.S. jobs.[2]5shrm.org
  • These figures should be used as indicators that policy commitments can shift posting practices and expand access, not as evidence that every role has been redesigned effectively or that outcomes are equitable without ongoing measurement.[23]172opm.gov
  • These figures should be used as indicators that policy commitments can shift posting practices and expand access, not as evidence that every role has been redesigned effectively or that outcomes are equitable without ongoing measurement.[23]172opm.gov
  • These figures should be used as indicators that policy commitments can shift posting practices and expand access, not as evidence that every role has been redesigned effectively or that outcomes are equitable without ongoing measurement.[23]172opm.gov
  • More than half of U.S. states have issued policy directives focused on skills-based hiring.our own page
  • In 25 states and one territory, those policies directed state hiring entities to reassess job requirements and prioritize skills, work history, and credentials rather than simply requiring a degree.[2]5shrm.org
  • In states with commitments, bachelor’s-degree requirements in state-government job postings fell by an estimated 2.5 percentage points for each additional year of policy exposure.[23]172opm.gov
  • A Harvard Business School and Burning Glass Institute study of more than 11,000 U.S. job postings from 2014 through 2023 found that hiring of candidates without degrees increased by 3.5 percentage points in jobs where degree requirements were removed.our own page
  • the U.S. federal government also expanded skills-based hiring across agencies.[43]157master-hr.com

Why removing degree requirements alone often has limited effects

  • Burning Glass Institute and Harvard Business School's 2024 analysis of more than 11,300 job postings estimated that only 1 in 700 hires in 2023 directly benefited from employers dropping degree requirements.our own page
  • That represented approximately 97,000 workers out of 77 million annual hires.[5]16naceweb.org
  • One source states that 45% of organizations removed bachelor’s-degree requirements for some roles in 2024, on top of 55% that had already done so in 2023; it also cites a poll of 800 U.S. employers in which about 80% preferred relevant experience to a college graduate credential.[11]171nationalskillscoalition.org
  • Public removal of degree requirements did not consistently translate into changed hiring outcomes: a Burning Glass Institute and Harvard Business School analysis found that only 37% of companies that publicly removed a degree requirement changed who they hired between 2014 and 2023.[1]7pin.com
  • The same collected source states that fewer than 1 in 700 new U.S. hires came from the corporate wave of dropped-degree announcements over that period.[1]7pin.com
  • A sourcing-platform dataset covering more than 37,000 recruiter-search sessions found that 79.7% of searches used skills filters, while 32.4% applied an education filter.[1]7pin.com
  • Indeed Hiring Lab’s 2024 analysis found that the share of job postings requiring a bachelor’s degree bottomed at 17.8% in early 2024, then trended upward later in the year.[1]7pin.com
  • The collected information characterizes this as a reversal in the prior decline of degree requirements.[1]7pin.com
  • A Harvard Business School and Burning Glass Institute analysis covering 2014 through 2023 found that fewer than 1 in 700 new U.S. hires came from employers’ corporate announcements that they had dropped degree requirements.[1]7pin.com
  • This gap between public commitments and realized hiring outcomes is a caveat for interpreting job-posting or policy-change data as proof of changes in actual screening practice.[1]7pin.com
  • The Harvard Business School/Burning Glass Institute Skills-Based Hiring study tracked publicly traded U.S. employers that removed bachelor’s-degree requirements from at least one job category between 2014 and 2023, then examined actual hiring outcomes.[1]7pin.com
  • Across the full study period, fewer than one in 700 new hires resulted from those policy changes.[1]7pin.com
  • - 37% of studied firms made a genuine hiring change, averaging about 18% more non-degree hires after the policy change.[1]7pin.com
  • - 45% announced degree-requirement removal but showed no meaningful change in hiring patterns, described as “in-name-only” adoption.[1]7pin.com
  • - 18% showed short-term gains and then backslid.[1]7pin.com
  • While the rise of skills-based hiring is undeniable, it doesn’t mean the transition has been easy. Many organizations are still straddling the old and new paradigms. A joint study by Harvard Business School and the Burning Glass Institute found that despite publicized commitments to drop degree requirements, actual hiring practices have been slow to catch up. In many cases, companies removed the degree ask from job postings but saw little change in who they ultimately hired. About 45% of companies that pledged to hire for skills made no meaningful changes in practice, and another 18% backtracked after initial efforts. This shows that simply eliminating credential requirements isn’t enough, as organizations must also change how they source, assess, and select job seekers.11testlify.com
  • One major challenge for recruiters is overcoming ingrained biases and habits. Data revealed that despite the broader adoption of skills-based hiring, over 60% of employers still admit to rejecting qualified candidates for lacking a college degree. Old heuristic bias dies hard. Hiring managers may feel uncertain about evaluating candidates without the familiar benchmarks of specific education or X years of experience. Internal resistance can crop up as HR teams might embrace the concept, but front-line hiring managers may question whether skills can substitute for years on the job. Building consensus for a skills-first mindset requires education and evidence.11testlify.com

AI hiring adoption and bias risks

  • When an AI filter touches every application, even a small per-applicant bias can compound into a large aggregate disparity.[3]20turbohire.co
  • Measure AI use, purpose, and oversight separately.our own page

Human oversight, auditing, and defensible records

  • Skills-based hiring programs should therefore document criteria, collect and analyze outcomes data, and provide transparent evaluations across agencies or business units to sustain support and identify inequitable results.[2]5shrm.org
  • Audit automated screening for disparate outcomes.our own page
  • Document whether demographic characteristics are removed before AI scoring, which data sources feed matching or ranking systems, and whether models are periodically tested for adverse impact.[3]20turbohire.co
  • Video and behavioral-AI assessments also require transparency because systems may analyze facial expressions, speech patterns, and communication style, while privacy and ethical concerns and emerging European and U.S. state requirements are increasing scrutiny of algorithmic scoring.[19]44onehour.digital
  • Black-box systems can also prevent employers from explaining why a candidate was rejected, leaving candidates unable to challenge the decision and employers exposed when they cannot justify an adverse employment decision.[28]116honorlock.com
  • A litigation-ready record should preserve model inputs and features, training and validation documentation, adverse-impact analyses, human-override practices, and vendor communications.[11]112cooley.com
  • Employers are advised to make AI a tool rather than the sole or substantial decision-maker in hiring, promotion, and termination decisions, with internal policy controls to ensure human involvement.[45]174huschblackwell.com
  • A 2026 HR compliance recommendation is a firm “human in the loop” mandate that prevents models from automatically rejecting a candidate without human review, because automated rejections can increase legal risk.[28]116honorlock.com
  • A 2026 HR compliance recommendation is a firm “human in the loop” mandate that prevents models from automatically rejecting a candidate without human review, because automated rejections can increase legal risk.[28]116honorlock.com
  • Providers and employers should inventory every tool that screens, ranks, recommends, schedules, scores, or routes candidates, including rules engines and third-party plugins, then classify each tool by its influence on outcomes, from informational to determinative.[11]112cooley.com
  • Providers and employers should inventory every tool that screens, ranks, recommends, schedules, scores, or routes candidates, including rules engines and third-party plugins, then classify each tool by its influence on outcomes, from informational to determinative.[11]112cooley.com
  • Recommended ongoing controls include adverse-impact testing at every selection stage, documented thresholds and escalation triggers, and preservation of prior model and configuration versions so decision changes can be explained over time.[11]112cooley.com

State requirements for automated hiring systems

  • California regulations effective October 1, 2025 apply to employers with five or more employees.[34]111affirmity.com
  • They require employers to retain automated-decision-system data and related employment records for four years from creation of the record or personnel action.[34][16]175111shrm.orgaffirmity.com
  • Anti-bias testing and similar proactive efforts may be considered in a discrimination claim, including the testing’s quality, efficacy, recency, scope, results, and the employer’s response to identified risks.[43]176manatt.com
  • Illinois HB 3773, effective January 1, 2026, requires notice when AI is used in recruitment, hiring, promotion, renewal of employment, training or apprenticeship selection, discharge, discipline, tenure, or employment terms and conditions; it also prohibits AI systems that use ZIP codes as proxies for protected classes.[45]174huschblackwell.com
  • Colorado’s AI Act, effective February 1, 2026, requires employers using covered consequential-decision systems to use reasonable care to avoid algorithmic discrimination, implement risk-management policies, conduct annual impact assessments, provide certain notices, and offer employees an opportunity to appeal adverse consequential decisions.[45]174huschblackwell.com
  • California requires covered employers and entities to retain relevant employment records for at least four years from the later of the record’s creation date or the date of the personnel action, replacing the prior two-year retention period.[10]117eklavvya.com
  • Covered records include selection criteria, applications, personnel records, and ADS data.[10]117eklavvya.com
  • “Automated-decision system data” includes data used in or resulting from an ADS, such as applicant or employee data and employment-decision outcomes, plus data used to develop or customize an ADS for a specific employer.[10]117eklavvya.com
  • Other guidance identifies dataset descriptors, scoring outputs, and audit findings as ADS-related records to preserve for four years.[49]177jacksonlewis.com
  • California does not mandate a bias audit, but FEHA makes the quality, efficacy, recency, and scope of anti-bias testing, its results, and the employer’s response to those results relevant to an employer’s defense against an ADS discrimination claim.[10]117eklavvya.com
  • The regulations allow employers to show that they performed anti-bias testing or comparable proactive efforts both before and after adopting an ADS.[10]117eklavvya.com
  • Guidance frames regular bias testing across protected groups, documentation of results, safeguards, human oversight, and post-deployment monitoring as central to defensible use, even where testing is not expressly compulsory.[47]118warden-ai.com
  • California’s pending SB 7, the “No Robo Bosses Act,” would require employers to disclose ADS use in employment decisions, maintain an updated inventory of ADS tools, notify job applicants when an ADS is used in hiring for the relevant position, and let workers access and correct ADS-collected or ADS-used data.[42]115hacklerflynnlaw.com
  • California’s separate automated decision-making technology (ADMT) privacy rules cover systems that replace or substantially replace human decision-making in processing personal data for significant decisions, including employment or independent-contracting opportunities and compensation.[46]126synhr.com
  • Where applicable, they require pre-use notice, an opt-out right, and access to specified information about the ADMT.[46]126synhr.com
  • California Privacy Risk Assessment rules also apply in part when CCPA-covered businesses use ADMT for a significant consumer decision; those rules took effect January 1, 2026, with compliance deadlines varying by when the covered processing began.[46]126synhr.com
  • Illinois amended its Human Rights Act so that, from January 1, 2026, employers must notify applicants or employees when AI will be used for hiring, recruitment, or other employment decisions; the amendment also expressly makes discrimination through AI tools unlawful.[46]126synhr.com
  • Illinois’ Artificial Intelligence Video Interview Act is a distinct regime focused on notice, consent, and related obligations when employers use AI to analyze video interviews.[11]112cooley.com
  • A separate Illinois requirement states that employers and employment agencies must notify candidates before machine-learning technology is used to make hiring decisions.[51]178ncsl.org
  • Some jurisdictions regulate particular technologies, such as Illinois video-interview analysis, while others embed employment obligations in broader AI, privacy, or anti-discrimination laws.[11][52]112179cooley.comdisa.com
  • New Jersey’s Division on Civil Rights has said that the New Jersey Law Against Discrimination can reach algorithmic discrimination, including disparate impact and barriers to accommodations.[11]112cooley.com
  • New Jersey regulations adopted in late 2025 reinforce that employment practices or policies with disparate impact on protected groups may violate the law even without discriminatory intent, including decisions made with automated employment decision tools.[46]126synhr.com
  • Colorado’s earlier AI Act framework covered high-risk systems used to make or substantially influence consequential decisions, including employment, and contemplated reasonable care against known or reasonably foreseeable algorithmic-discrimination risks, documentation, and notices.[11]112cooley.com
  • The collected material reports subsequent changes: Colorado repealed and replaced the earlier Act with a law governing ADMT that makes, guides, or assists consequential decisions, including employment access, eligibility, or compensation.[46]126synhr.com

Federal enforcement and validation expectations

  • EEOC guidance emphasizes the risk that these tools can reinforce existing bias or perpetuate discrimination unintentionally and that employers should assess disparate impact and whether a tool is job-related and consistent with business necessity.[56]180linkedin.com
  • Employers considering AI-based selection algorithms should conduct adverse-impact studies and be prepared to produce validation studies for their selection procedures.[54]100madisonapproach.com
  • This is particularly salient for federal contractors, given OFCCP’s impact-ratio analysis and statistical-testing approach.[54]100madisonapproach.com
